Dispute Over a Condition of Quality in Salam Contract: Whose Statement is Accepted?
Ruling on whose statement is accepted in a salam quality dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of muta'annit to determine whose statement is accepted in a salam contract dispute, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A party who obstinately denies a benefit for himself is not believed; the defendant's statement is accepted even if he denies the contract's validity.

In salam contracts, precise description of the commodity is essential to avoid gharar. When a dispute arises over whether a condition of low quality was stipulated, the court must decide whose word is taken. This passage from al-Hidayah presents the ruling and the reasoning behind it, highlighting an important intra-Hanafi difference that turns on the concept of muta'annit (obstinate denial).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
In the given case, the seller (muslim ilayhi) claims he stipulated low quality (radiyy); the buyer (rabb al-salam) denies any such condition. Note who is claiming something beyond the default.
The buyer's denial means he insists the contract was for default (good) quality.
2
Determine the default benefit
In salam, the muslam fihi (commodity) usually exceeds the capital in value. A condition of low quality benefits the buyer by reducing his obligation. Thus, the buyer's denial of the condition is against his own benefit.
The buyer gains by not having to accept low quality, so denying the condition is contrary to his interest.
3
Apply the concept of muta'annit
A party who denies something that benefits him is considered obstinate (muta'annit). His statement is not accepted because it contradicts apparent custom. Hence the seller's statement is taken.
The principle prevents a party from benefiting from his own stubbornness.
4
Consider the reverse case
If the buyer claims the condition and the seller denies, the reasoning differs. Abu Hanifa says the buyer's statement is accepted because he claims validity (the contract is valid with the condition), which is the default. The Sahibayn say the seller's statement is accepted because he is the denier.
The default is that contracts are valid (sihhat al-aqd), so the one asserting validity is essentially the defendant.
5
Understand the preferred view
The commentator of Ashraf al-Hidayah favors Abu Hanifa's view because it looks beyond formal roles to the substantive reality: the buyer's claim of validity aligns with the norm, making him the true defendant.
This shows Hanafi fiqh's attention to maqasid over mere formalities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن اسلم الى رجل دراهم فى كر حنطة فقال المسلم اليه شرطت رديا وقال رب السلم لم تشترط شيئا فالقول قول المسلم اليه لان رب السلم متعنت فى انكاره الصحة لان المسلم فيه يربو على رأس المال في العادة وفى عكسه قالوا يجب ان يكون القول لرب السلم عند ابي حنيفة لأنه يدعى الصحة وان كان صاحبه منكرا وعندهما القول للمسلم اليه لانه منكر وان انكر المصحة وسنقرره من بعد ان شاء الله تعالى
And from one who gave dirhams to a man in salam for a kurr of wheat, and the muslim ilayhi said, 'I stipulated it to be of low quality,' and the rabb al-salam said, 'You did not stipulate anything,' then the statement is the statement of the muslim ilayhi, because the rabb al-salam is obstinate in his denial of validity, because the muslam fihi usually exceeds the capital. And in its opposite, they said that the statement should be of the rabb al-salam according to Abu Hanifa, because he claims validity even though his companion denies it, and according to them (the two) the statement is of the muslim ilayhi because he is the denier even if he denies the soundness. And we will establish it later, God willing.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidayah, commented upon in Ashraf al-Hidayah, addresses a dispute in a salam contract where the seller (muslim ilayhi) claims he stipulated the commodity to be of low quality (radiyy), while the buyer (rabb al-salam) denies any such condition. The ruling is that the seller's statement is accepted because the buyer is acting obstinately (muta'annit) by denying a condition that benefits him—since the muslam fihi typically exceeds the capital in value. In the reverse scenario, where the buyer claims the condition and the seller denies it, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer's statement is accepted because he claims validity (the contract is valid with the condition), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller's statement is accepted because he is the denier. The commentator indicates that the view of Abu Hanifa is preferable and will be established later.

Key Terms
متعنت (muta'annit): A person who obstinately denies something that is beneficial or a right, acting out of spite or stubbornness.
ردي (radiyy): Low quality; a description that must be stated in a salam contract to ensure lack of ambiguity.
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The buyer/purchaser in a salam contract who pays the capital in advance.
مسلم اليه (muslim ilayhi): The seller in a salam contract who receives the capital and is obligated to deliver the commodity at a future date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, if the seller (muslim ilayhi) claims he stipulated a condition of low quality (radiyy) and the buyer (rabb al-salam) denies any condition, whose statement is accepted and why?
A) The seller's, because he is the defendant.
B) The buyer's, because he is the plaintiff.
C) The seller's, because the buyer is muta'annit.
D) The buyer's, because the seller is muta'annit.

Key Takeaway
In a salam contract dispute over a quality condition, the party who is muta'annit (obstinately denying a benefit) is not believed; the defendant's statement is accepted, even if he denies validity.

Whose Statement Is Accepted in a Salam Term Dispute
Ruling on whose statement is accepted when disputing the existence of a fixed term in salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted in a dispute over a fixed term in a salam contract and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In a binding salam contract, default is validity; the party claiming validity is believed, not the one claiming invalidity.

When two parties to a salam contract dispute whether a fixed term (ajal) was stipulated, the law must determine whose word is taken. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi approach, focusing on an important intra-madhhab difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The ruling hinges on the binding nature of salam versus the non-binding nature of mudaraba, and the default presumption of validity when both parties agree on a single contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dispute scenarios
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) seller denies term, buyer affirms; (2) seller affirms term, buyer denies. The first scenario has a unanimous ruling; the second has an ikhtilaf.
Scenario 1 is straightforward; the dispute is in scenario 2.
2
State the unanimous ruling for scenario 1
When the seller (muslim ilayhi) says no term existed and the buyer (rabb al-salam) says it did, the buyer's statement is accepted. The seller is considered obstinate (mutaʿannit) for denying the buyer's right.
The benefit of the term lies with the buyer (delivery date).
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf in scenario 2
In the reverse case, the Sahibayn accept the buyer's statement (the denier of validity) by analogy to mudaraba. Abu Hanifa accepts the seller's statement (the claimant of validity) because salam is a binding contract where default is validity.
The key distinction: binding (lazim) vs. non-binding (ja'iz) contract.
4
Evaluate the Sahibayn's analogy to mudaraba
In mudaraba, if the capital owner claims a condition limiting profit, his statement is accepted because he denies the worker's entitlement. The Sahibayn apply this to salam: the buyer who admits the term acknowledges a right upon himself, so his statement should be taken.
But mudaraba is non-binding, so the reasoning may not transfer.
5
Confirm the preferred Hanafi position
The commentator of Ashraf al-Hidayah favors Imam Abu Hanifa's view because it respects the binding nature of salam and the principle that parties who agree on one contract are presumed to agree on validity.
Mufta bihi: In a term dispute, the claim of validity (existence of term) is accepted from the one who affirms it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال المسلم اليه لم يكن له اجل وقال رب السلم بل كان له اجل فالقول قول رب السلم لأن المسلم اليه متعنت فى انكاره حقا له وهو الاجل والفساد لعدم الاجل غير متيقن لمكان الاجتهاد فلا يعتبر النفع في رد رأس المال بخلاف عدم الوصف وفى عكسه القول لرب السلم عندهما لانه يتكر حقا عليه فيكون القول قوله وان انكر الصحة كرب المال اذا قال للمضارب شرطت لك نصف الربح الا عشرة وقال المضارب لا بل شرطت لى نصف الربح فالقول لرب المال لانه ينكر استحقاق الربح وان انكر المصحة وعند ابي حنيفة القول للمسلم اليه لانه يدعى الصحة وقد اتفقا على عقد واحد فكانا متفقين على الصحة ظاهرا بخلاف مسألة المضاربة ولانه ليس بلازم فلا يعتبر الاختلاف فيه فبقى مجرد دعوى استحقاق الربح اما السلم فلازم فصار الاصل ان من خرج كلامه تعنتا فالقول لصاحبه بالاتفاق وان خرج خصومة ووقع الاتفاق على عقد واحد فالقول لمدعى الصحة عنده وعندهما للمنكر وإن انكر الصحة
And if the muslim ilayhi said, 'There was no fixed term,' and the rabb al-salam said, 'Rather there was a fixed term,' then the statement is the statement of the rabb al-salam, because the muslim ilayhi is obstinate in his denial of a right of his, namely the term, and the invalidity due to absence of term is not certain because of ijtihad, so the benefit in returning the capital is not considered, unlike the absence of description. In its opposite, the statement is of the rabb al-salam according to them (the two), because he acknowledges a right upon himself, so his statement is taken even if he denies the soundness, like the case of the owner of capital who says to the mudarib, 'I stipulated half the profit for you except ten,' and the mudarib says, 'No, rather you stipulated half the profit for me,' then the statement is of the owner of capital because he denies entitlement to profit even if he denies the soundness. According to Abu Hanifa, the statement is of the muslim ilayhi because he claims soundness, and they have agreed on one contract, so they are apparently in agreement on soundness, unlike the mudaraba case, and because it (mudaraba) is not binding, so the difference in it is not considered, so it remains a mere claim of entitlement to profit. As for salam, it is binding, so the principle becomes: one whose speech emerges as obstinate, then the statement is of his companion by agreement; and if it emerges as a dispute and they have agreed on one contract, then according to him (Abu Hanifa) the statement is of the one claiming validity, and according to them (the two) for the denier, even if he denies the validity.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidayah, interpreted by Ashraf al-Hidayah, deals with a dispute over the existence of a fixed term (ajal) in a salam contract. If the seller (muslim ilayhi) denies the term and the buyer (rabb al-salam) affirms it, the buyer's statement is accepted because the seller is obstinate in denying the buyer's right (the term benefits the buyer). The validity does not depend heavily on the term due to ijtihad. In the reverse case, the Sahibayn analogize to a mudaraba dispute: the one who acknowledges a right upon himself (here the rabb al-salam admitting the term) is believed. They also note that in mudaraba, the contract is not binding, so the dispute is treated as a mere claim. However, Abu Hanifa distinguishes: in salam, a binding contract, the parties agreed on a single contract, so the default is validity; thus, the one who claims validity (the muslim ilayhi claiming the term existed, making the contract valid) is believed. The commentator supports Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
اجل (ajal): The fixed term or delivery date in a salam contract, which is necessary for validity.
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, sharing profits.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The owner of capital in a mudarabah or investment context.
مضارب (mudarib): The worker/manager in a mudarabah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over a fixed term (ajal) in a salam contract where the seller (muslim ilayhi) claims a term was stipulated and the buyer (rabb al-salam) denies, according to the mufta bihi (preferred view), whose statement is accepted?
A) The buyer's, because he denies a right upon himself.
B) The seller's, because he claims validity and the contract is binding.
C) The buyer's, because the seller is obstinate.
D) The seller's, because he acknowledges a right upon himself.

Key Takeaway
In a dispute over a term in a salam contract, the binding nature of the contract leads to a default presumption of validity, so the party claiming validity (the seller, if he affirms the term) is believed; this is the preferred view over the Sahibayn's analogy to non-binding mudaraba.

Dispute on Term (Ajal) in Salam: Intra-Hanafi Ruling
Resolves who speaks first when term is disputed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted when the term (ajal) is disputed in a salam contract and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement in the reverse scenario.

Core Idea
When a party denies his own benefit, his denial is obstinate and not accepted; but in genuine disputes, Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on who prevails.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a procedural question in salam contracts: whose word is taken when the parties disagree about whether a term was specified. The answer hinges on whether the denial is obstinate (ta'annut) or a genuine dispute. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the reverse scenario, where muslam ilayh claims validity and rabb al-salam denies it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
In the first scenario, muslam ilayh denies that a term was agreed, and rabb al-salam affirms it. Determine the legal consequence.
2
Apply the ruling for the first scenario
All Hanafis agree that rabb al-salam's word is accepted because muslam ilayh is obstinate in denying his own right.
The invalidity of the contract due to lack of term is not certain due to ijtihad.
3
Consider the reverse scenario
Muslam ilayh claims the contract is valid (with term), and rabb al-salam denies it. Note the disagreement.
This is where the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf occurs.
4
State the disagreement: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa accepts muslam ilayh's word because they both agreed on the same contract and he claims validity. Sahibayn accept rabb al-salam's word because he denies a right against himself.
The analogy with mudaraba is used to illustrate the difference.
5
Derive the general principle
The text concludes: if speech is obstinate, the other's word is accepted; if it is a genuine dispute and both agree on one contract, then Abu Hanifa accepts the pro-validity claimant, Sahibayn accept the denier.
This principle can be applied to other similar disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال المسلم اليه لم يكن له اجل وقال رب السلم بل كان له اجل فالقول قول رب السلم لأن المسلم اليه متعنت فى انكاره حقا له وهو الاجل والفساد لعدم الاجل غير متيقن لمكان الاجتهاد فلا يعتبر النفع في رد رأس المال بخلاف عدم الوصف وفى عكسه القول لرب السلم عندهما لانه يتكر حقا عليه فيكون القول قوله وان انكر الصحة كرب المال اذا قال للمضارب شرطت لك نصف الربح الا عشرة وقال المضارب لا بل شرطت لى نصف الربح فالقول لرب المال لانه ينكر استحقاق الربح وان انكر المصحة وعند ابي حنيفة القول للمسلم اليه لانه يدعى الصحة وقد اتفقا على عقد واحد فكانا متفقين على الصحة ظاهرا بخلاف مسألة المضاربة ولانه ليس بلازم فلا يعتبر الاختلاف فيه فبقى مجرد دعوى استحقاق الربح اما السلم فلازم فصار الاصل ان من خرج كلامه تعنتا فالقول لصاحبه بالاتفاق وان خرج خصومة ووقع الاتفاق على عقد واحد فالقول لمدعى الصحة عنده وعندهما للمنكر وإن انكر الصحة
ترجمہ اور اگر مسلم الیہ نے کہا کہ ( مسلم فیہ ادا کرنے کے لئے کوئی میعاد نہیں تھی اور رب السلام نے کہا بلکہ اس کے لیے میعاد تھی تو رب السلم کا قول قبول ہوگا کیونکہ مسلم الیہ اپنے حق یعنی میعاد کا انکار کرنے میں منعت ( سرکش ) ہے۔ اور میعاد نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے فساد غیر یقینی ہے کیونکہ (اس میں ) اختلاف اور اجتہاد ہے۔ پس رأس المال واپس کرنے میں نفع معتبر نہ ہو گا۔ بر خلاف عدم بیان وصف کے اور اس کے برعکس صورت میں صاحبین کے نزدیک رب السلم کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وہ اپنے اوپر ایک حق ہونے سے انکار کرتا ہے لہذا اس کا قول قبول ہوگا ۔ اگر چہ وہ صحت مسلم کا منکر ہے۔ جیسے جب رب المال نے مضارب سے کہا کہ میں نے تیرے لئے علاوہ دس درہم کے آدھے نفع کی شرط کی تھی اور مضارب نے کہا نہیں، بلکہ تو نے میرے لیے آدھے نفع کی شرط کی تھی تو رب المال کا قول قبول ہوگا کیونکہ وہ استحقاق نفع کا منکر ہے اگر چہ صحت مقاربت کا انکار کرتا ہے اور ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک مسلم الیہ کا قول قبول ہو گا ۔ کیونکہ وہ صحت سلم کا دعویٰ کرتا ہے حالانکہ دونوں ایک ہی عقد پر متفق ہیں تو بظاہر دونوں اس کی صحت پر متفق ہوئے برخلاف مسئلہ مضاربت کے اور اس لیے کہ مضاربت لازم نہیں ہوتی ہے پس اس میں اختلاف معتبر نہ ہوگا اور منض نفع کے استحقاق کا دعوئی باقی رہا۔ رہی بیع سلم تو وہ ایک عقد لازم ہے پس قاعدہ کلیہ یہ ہوا کہ جس کا کلام از راہ سرکشی نکلا تو اس کے ساتھی کا قول بالا تفاق معتبر ہو گا۔ اور اگر اس کا کلام از راہ خصومت نکلا اور ایک عقد پر اتفاق واقع ہوا تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک مدعی صحت کا قول معتبر ہو گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک منکر کا قول معتبر ہو گا اگر چہ وہ منکر صحت ہو۔
And if the muslam ilayh said, 'There was no term for it,' and rabb al-salam said, 'Rather, there was a term for it,' then the saying is the saying of rabb al-salam, because the muslam ilayh is obstinate in denying his own right, which is the term; and invalidity due to absence of term is not certain because of ijtihad; so benefit is not considered in returning the capital, contrary to the omission of description. And in its opposite, the saying is for rabb al-salam according to them (the two), because he denies a right against himself, so his saying is his saying, even if he denies validity. Like when rabb al-mal said to the mudarib, 'I stipulated for you half the profit except ten,' and the mudarib said, 'No, but you stipulated for me half the profit,' then the saying is of rabb al-mal because he denies entitlement to profit, even if he denies validity. And according to Abu Hanifa, the saying is for the muslam ilayh, because he claims validity, and they have agreed upon one contract, so they are apparently agreed upon validity, unlike the mudaraba case; and because it is not binding, difference is not considered in it, so mere claim of entitlement to profit remains. As for salam, it is binding, so the principle became: Whoever's words come out as obstinacy, then the saying is to his companion by agreement; and if they come out as dispute and they agree upon one contract, then according to him (Abu Hanifa) the saying is for the claimant of validity, and according to them (the two) for the denier, even if he denies validity. Translation: And if the muslam ilayh said that there was no term for delivering the muslam fihi, and rabb al-salam said rather there was a term, then the saying of rabb al-salam will be accepted because the muslam ilayh is obstinate in denying his own right, i.e., the term. And invalidity due to absence of term is not certain because there is difference and ijtihad therein. So benefit in returning the capital will not be considered. Contrary to omission of description. And in its opposite case, according to the two (Sahibayn), the saying of rabb al-salam will be accepted, because he denies a right against himself, so his saying will be accepted, even if he denies the validity of salam. Like when rabb al-mal said to the mudarib, 'I stipulated for you half the profit except ten dirhams,' and the mudarib said, 'No, but you stipulated for me half the profit,' then the saying of rabb al-mal will be accepted because he denies entitlement to profit, even if he denies the validity of mudaraba. And according to Abu Hanifa, the saying of the muslam ilayh will be accepted, because he claims validity, and they both agree on one contract, so apparently they both agree on its validity, unlike the mudaraba case; and because mudaraba is not binding, difference in it is not considered, and only the claim of entitlement to profit remains. As for salam, it is a binding contract, so the general principle is: Whoever's speech comes out as obstinacy, then his companion's saying is accepted by agreement. And if his speech comes out as dispute and they agree on one contract, then according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) the saying of the claimant of validity is accepted, and according to the two (Sahibayn) the saying of the denier is accepted, even if he denies validity.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a dispute in a salam contract. The issue: When muslam ilayh denies that a term (ajal) was specified for delivery, and rabb al-salam affirms it, all Hanafis agree that rabb al-salam's word is accepted. The reason is that muslam ilayh is denying a right that benefits him (the term allows him more time), so his denial is tantamount to obstinacy (ta'annut). His denial is rejected. Furthermore, the invalidity of the contract due to lack of term is not certain because there is ijtihad (Imam Shafi'i considers salam without a term valid). Thus, muslam ilayh cannot claim that he benefits by returning the capital. The principle is: whoever's speech is obstinate, his companion's word prevails. Then the text discusses the reverse scenario: muslam ilayh claims validity and rabb al-salam denies it. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), rabb al-salam's word is accepted because he is denying a right against himself (he denies validity which would require him to take the capital). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, muslam ilayh's word is accepted because he claims validity, and since both agree on the same contract, they are presumed to agree on its validity as well—unlike mudaraba which is non-binding. The general rule derived: if the denial is obstinate (ta'annut), the other's word is accepted unanimously; if it is a genuine dispute (khusuma) and both agree on the same contract, then Imam Abu Hanifa accepts the word of the one claiming validity, while the Sahibayn accept the word of the denier.

Key Terms
مسلم الیہ (muslam ilayh): The seller in a salam contract who receives the capital and is obligated to deliver the specified commodity (muslam fihi) at a future date.
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The buyer in a salam contract who pays the capital upfront to receive the commodity later.
متعنت (muta'annit): One who is obstinate or stubborn, denying something that is clearly to his own benefit, thus his denial is not accepted.
اجل (ajal): The specified term or delivery date in a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reverse scenario (muslam ilayh claims validity, rabb al-salam denies), what is the position of the Sahibayn?
A) They accept the word of muslam ilayh.
B) They accept the word of rabb al-salam because he denies a right against himself.
C) They consider the contract invalid due to dispute.
D) They require both parties to take an oath.

Key Takeaway
In a salam contract, if one party denies his own benefit, his statement is rejected; but in genuine disputes, Abu Hanifa favors the one claiming validity while the Sahibayn favor the denier.

Dispute on Term in Salam: Buyer's Word Accepted
Ruling on disagreement about term in salam contract with objection and contrast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dispute about the term in a salam contract, apply the principle of obstinacy (ta'annut), and distinguish it from the case of a dispute about description.

Core Idea
When seller denies term, buyer's word prevails due to obstinacy; voidness is uncertain.

In a salam contract, the term (ajal) is a crucial element for validity according to the Hanafi school. If a dispute arises between the buyer and seller about whether a term was agreed, the school provides a clear ruling based on the principle of obstinacy. This lesson unpacks that ruling, its rationale, and how it differs from similar disputes about description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
Recognize that the dispute is about the term (ajal) in a salam contract. The seller denies it was fixed; the buyer affirms it.
Term is a condition for validity in Hanafi, but not in Shafi'i.
2
Determine who is denying a right
The term is a right of the seller (to deliver later) and a benefit (he can keep the capital longer). By denying it, he is denying his own right.
Denying one's own right indicates obstinacy (ta'annut).
3
Apply the principle of obstinacy
The speech of an obstinate person is rejected. Therefore, the seller's statement is rejected, and the buyer's statement is accepted.
This is the unanimous Hanafi position.
4
Address the objection
One might argue that denying the term voids the contract, benefiting the seller (he keeps the commodity). But the voidness is not certain due to ikhtilaf (Shafi'i allows termless salam). Hence, the denial does not guarantee profit, so obstinacy remains.
Uncertain voidness means the seller does not necessarily profit.
5
Contrast with description dispute
If the dispute is about specification (wasf), the voidness is certain and undisputed. Hence the denier (buyer) is obstinate, and the seller's word is accepted.
This contrast highlights the role of certainty in determining obstinacy.
6
Note the general principle and intra-Hanafi difference
General rule: When a party speaks out of obstinacy, the opponent's word is accepted. When both agree on the contract but differ on its validity, Imam Abu Hanifa believes the one claiming validity; the Sahibayn believe the denier.
This broader principle applies beyond salam.

والفساد لعدم الاجل ...... الخ - سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ مسلم الیہ، میعادکا انکار کرنے میں مشقت نہیں ہے، اس لئے کہ میعاد کا انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے عقد سلم فاسد ہو گیا اور عقد فاسد ہونے کی وجہ سے مسلم الیہ ، راس المال واپس کرے گا اور جب مسلم الیہ راس المال واپس کرے گا تو مسلم فیہ اس کے واسطے سلامت رہے گی اور سابق میں گذر چکا کہ مسلم فیہ بہر صورت رأس المال سے بہتر اور انفع ہوتی ہے تو گویا مسلم الیہ نے میعاد کا انکار کر کے کسی نفع بخش چیز کا انکار نہیں کیا بلکہ نفع بخش چیز یعنی مسلم فیہ کو اپنے لیے محفوظ کر لیا اور ضرر رساں چیز یعنی راس المال کو واپس کر دیا اور ایسا کرنے والے کو متعنت نہیں کہا جاتا ، پس جب مسلم الیہ محنت نہیں ہوا تو اس کا کلام رد بھی نہ ہونا چاہیے تھا، حالانکہ آپ نے فرمایا ہے کہ مسلم الیہ کا کلام مردود ہے۔

Chapter of Salam. Translation: And if the seller (muslim ilayh) said that there was no term for delivering the sold commodity, and the buyer (rabb al-salam) said rather there was a term for it, then the statement of the buyer will be accepted because the seller is obstinate in denying his own right, i.e., the term. And the corruption due to lack of term is not certain because there is difference of opinion and ijtihad. Therefore, returning the capital does not give a profit. Contrary to the case of not specifying a description, and in the opposite scenario, according to the Sahibayn, the statement of the buyer is accepted because he denies a right upon himself, so his statement is accepted even if he denies the validity of the salam. Like when the owner of capital said to the mudarib: 'I stipulated half the profit for you excluding ten dirhams,' and the mudarib said, 'No, rather you stipulated half the profit for me,' then the statement of the owner of capital is accepted because he denies the right to profit, even if he denies the validity of the mudarabah. And according to Abu Hanifa, the statement of the seller is accepted because he claims the validity of the salam, and both agree on the same contract, so apparently both agree on its validity, contrary to the issue of mudarabah. And because mudarabah is not binding, so the difference is not considered, and the claim of the right to profit remains. As for the salam sale, it is a binding contract. So the general rule is: whoever speaks out of obstinacy, his companion's statement is accepted by consensus. And if his speech comes out of dispute and they agree on one contract, then according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) the statement of the one claiming validity is accepted, and according to the Sahibayn the statement of the denier is accepted, even if he denies validity.

Explanation: The scenario is that if the seller and buyer disagreed about fixing the term, such that the seller said no term was fixed for delivering the sold commodity and the buyer said a term was fixed, then in this case, by consensus of the Hanafis, the statement of the buyer is accepted; this is also the statement of Imam Shafi'i. Because when the seller denied his right and beneficial thing, i.e., the term, his speech became obstinate and he was stubborn. It has already passed that the speech of an obstinate and stubborn person is rejected. Therefore, the seller's speech is rejected. And when the seller's speech is rejected, his companion's, i.e., the buyer's, speech is accepted.

'And the corruption due to lack of term...' from here is an answer to a question. The question is: The seller is not being difficult by denying the term because by denying the term, the salam contract becomes corrupt, and due to the corrupt contract, the seller will return the capital, and when the seller returns the capital, the sold commodity remains safe for him, and it has passed that the sold commodity is always better and more beneficial than the capital. So it is as if the seller, by denying the term, did not deny a beneficial thing but rather kept the beneficial thing, i.e., the sold commodity, for himself and returned the harmful thing, i.e., the capital, and one who does such is not called obstinate. So when the seller is not obstinate, his speech should not be rejected, whereas you said that the seller's speech is rejected.

Answer: The answer is that the corruption of the salam contract due to lack of term is not certain. Because this issue is disputed; indeed, according to Imam Shafi'i, a spot salam contract (without a term) is valid, even if other scholars do not permit it. So since in the case of denying the term, the corruption is not certain, therefore returning the capital is not necessary due to the denial of the term. And when returning the capital is not necessary, then by this denial, the seller does not gain full profit. Yes, the term, which is the apparent right and benefit of the seller, by denying it, the seller is certainly obstinate and stubborn. And the speech of an obstinate person is rejected, so the seller's speech is rejected. And when the seller's speech is rejected, the buyer's speech is accepted. Contrary to this, if the seller said, 'I stipulated a description for the sold commodity,' and the buyer said, 'No description was stated for the sold commodity,' then in this case the seller's statement is accepted because the corruption of the salam sale due to the absence of description is certain and undisputed, so the buyer, by denying the description, is considered obstinate and rebellious, and due to being obstinate, his statement is not accepted; rather, the seller's statement is accepted.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with a dispute in a salam contract regarding whether a fixed term (ajal) was agreed upon. The author presents the ruling: if the seller (muslim ilayh) denies a term and the buyer (rabb al-salam) affirms it, the buyer's word is accepted by unanimous Hanafi opinion. The reasoning is that the seller is being obstinate (mutamattid) by denying his own right and benefit (the term), and the speech of an obstinate person is rejected. A question arises: since denying the term would render the contract void (fasad) and require the seller to return the capital while keeping the commodity, he actually benefits from the denial, so why is he considered obstinate? The answer is that the fasad is not certain because there is a difference of opinion (Imam Shafi'i allows salam without a term). Thus, the denial does not guarantee profit, so the seller remains obstinate regarding the term itself. The passage contrasts this with a dispute about specifying a description (wasf): if the seller claims a description was stipulated and the buyer denies it, the seller's word is accepted because the invalidity due to missing description is certain and undisputed, making the buyer the obstinate party. The author also introduces a general principle: if one party speaks out of obstinacy, his opponent's statement is accepted; if they both agree on the same contract but differ on its validity, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the one who claims validity is believed, while according to the Sahibayn the denier is believed.

Key Terms
مسلم الیہ (muslim ilayh): The seller in a salam contract, who receives the capital and is obligated to deliver the commodity at a future date.
رب السلام (rabb al-salam): The buyer in a salam contract, who pays the capital upfront.
میعاد (mīʿād): The fixed term or deadline for delivery in a salam contract.
محنت / متعنت (taʿannut / mutaʿannit): Obstinacy or stubbornness as a legal principle: the speech of a person who is obstinate in denying a right or benefit is rejected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, if the seller denies that a term was agreed and the buyer affirms it, whose statement is accepted according to the unanimous Hanafi position?
A) The seller's statement is accepted because he is the one delivering the commodity.
B) The buyer's statement is accepted because the seller is considered obstinate in denying his own right.
C) They must both provide witnesses to resolve the dispute.
D) The contract is automatically void due to the disagreement.

Key Takeaway
In disputes about the term of a salam contract, the buyer's word is accepted because the seller's denial of his own right makes him obstinate; this ruling holds despite the possibility that denying the term could void the contract, because the voidness is not certain.

Reverse Dispute on Term in Salam: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on crediting claims in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the reverse salam term dispute and explain the underlying reasoning and practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
When seller claims a term and buyer denies it, Abu Hanifa accepts seller's statement; Sahibayn accept buyer's statement.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi difference on a procedural issue in salam contracts: who is believed when the seller claims a term was agreed and the buyer denies it. The Sahibayn analogize to mudarabah, while Abu Hanifa relies on the apparent state of the parties as Muslims who intend valid contracts. Understanding this dispute sharpens your skill in distinguishing legal arguments based on the nature of the contract (binding vs. non-binding).

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify the exact dispute: In a salam contract, the seller (muslam ilayh) claims that a term (ajal) was agreed for delivery, while the buyer (rabb al-salam) denies that any term was stipulated.
This is the reverse of the earlier scenario where the seller denies the term.
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
State the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa (seller's statement is accepted) and the Sahibayn (buyer's statement is accepted). Recognize that this is an ikhtilaf within the school.
The Sahibayn are Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Follow their qiyas to mudarabah: In mudarabah, if the capital owner denies an additional profit condition, his statement is accepted because he is the denier of the mudarib's claimed right. Similarly here, the buyer denies the seller's claimed right to a term, so the buyer's statement is accepted.
The key principle: the denier's word is taken when the claimant has no proof.
4
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine his distinction: Salam is a binding contract (lazim), not like mudarabah which is non-binding (ja'iz). Both parties agree it is a single contract; the seller claims validity (by affirming the term) and the buyer claims invalidity. Since both are Muslims, their natural disposition is to avoid invalid contracts, so the apparent state supports the seller's claim of validity.
Abu Hanifa's proof relies on the apparent state (zahir al-hal) and the nature of the contract.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: Under Abu Hanifa, the contract is valid and the seller can enforce the term. Under the Sahibayn, the buyer's denial is accepted, so no term is established and the contract may be voidable due to dispute on an essential term.
The mufta bihi is not specified in this passage; later practice may favor the Sahibayn's view as it aligns with the general principle that the denier's statement is accepted.

صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر متن کا مسئلہ بر عکس ہو گیا یعنی مسلم الیہ نے میعاد کا دعوی کیا اور رب السلم نے انکار کیا تو صاحبین کے نزد یک رب السلم کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک مسلم الیہ کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ رب السلم ایک ایسے حق کا انکار کرتا ہے جو مسلم الیہ کا رب السلم پر ہے، یعنی مسلم فی ادا کرنے کے لیے میعاد کا ہونا مسلم الیہ کا حق ہے اور اس میں مسلم الیہ کا نفع بھی ہے بایں طور کہ مسلم الیہ اس مدت میں رأس المال سے خرید وفروخت کر کے اچھا خاصا نفع کما سکتا ہے پس رب السلام نے میعاد کا انکار کر کے مسلم الیہ کے مذکورہ حق اور نفع کا انکار کیا ہے اگر چہ اس کے ضمن میں بیع سلم صحیح ہونے کا انکار بھی ہو گیا اور مسلم الیہ نے اپنے اس حق اور نفع کا دعوی کیا ہے اور مسلم الیہ یعنی مدعی کے پاس چونکہ مینہ موجود نہیں ہے اس لیے منکر یعنی رب السلم کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اور اس کی مثال ایسی ہے جیسے عقد مضاربت میں رب المال نے مضارب سے کہا کہ میں نے تیرے لئے آدھے نفع کی شرط لگائی تھی علاوہ دس درہم کے یعنی حاصل شدہ نفع میں سے خاص طور پر دس درہم میرے لیے ہونگے اور باقی نفع دونوں کے درمیان آدھا آدھا ہوگا ۔ اور مضارب نے کہا کہ دس درہم کے استثناء کی کوئی شرط نہیں تھی بلکہ شرط یہ تھی کہ پورا نفع ہم دونوں کے درمیان نصف نصف ہوگا ، تو اس صورت میں رب المال کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے کیونکہ رب المال اس زیادتی نفع کا منکر ہے جس کا مضارب مستحق ہے یعنی رب المال نے جس دس دراہم کو اپنے لئے خاص کیا ہے مضارب کہتا ہے کہ میں ان میں بھی برابر کا شریک ہوں اور میرا حق ان میں بھی ثابت ہے مگر رب المال مضارب کے اس حق کا انکار کرتا ہے اگر چہ رب المال کے اس انکار اور دس درہم کے استثناء کی وجہ سے عقد مضاربت فاسد ہو جاتا ہے پس باوجود یکہ رب المال کے قول سے عقد مضاربت میں فساد لازم آتا ہے مگر چونکہ وہ اپنے اوپر مضارب کے لیے زیادتی نفع کا منکر ہے اور منکر کا قول مدعی کے پاس ہینہ نہ ہونے کی صورت میں معتبر ہوتا ہے اس لیے اس صورت میں منکر یعنی رب المال کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عقد سلم کی صورت میں رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ دونوں ایک عقد پر متفق ہیں ۔ کیونکہ سلم ، عقد واعد ہے۔ اور شرائط سلم میں سے کسی شرط کا انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے عقد سلم فاسد ہو جاتا ہے دوسرا عقد نہیں ہو جاتا پس مسئلہ مذکورہ میں دونوں کا عقد واحد پر اتفاق ہوا لیکن صحت اور فساد میں اختلاف ہے اس طور پر کہ مسلم الیہ اجل اور میعاد کا دعوی کر کے سلم کے صحیح ہونے کا مدعی ہے اور ا میعاد کا انکار کر کے اس کے فاسد ہونے کا مدعی ہے اور ظاہر حال اس پر شاہد ہے کہ دونوں سلم کے صحیح ہونے پر متفق ہوں کیونکہ رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ دونوں مسلمان ہیں اور مسلمان کا ظاہر حال اس کا متقاضی ہے کہ وہ عقد فاسد سے احتراز کرے۔ اور عقد صحیح کا ارتکاب کرے۔ پس رب السلم کے مسلمان ہونے سے بظاہر معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ وہ بھی عقد صحیح کا مدعی ہے اور وہ عقد صحیح کا مدعی اس وقت ہو
The author of Hidayah says that if the issue of the text is reversed, i.e., the seller claimed a term and the buyer denied it, then according to the Sahibayn the buyer's statement is accepted, and according to Imam Abu Hanifa the seller's statement is accepted. The proof of the Sahibayn is that the buyer denies a right that the seller has upon the buyer, i.e., the existence of a term for delivering the sold commodity is the seller's right and there is benefit for the seller in it, in that the seller can trade with the capital during that period and earn good profit. So the buyer, by denying the term, denied the aforementioned right and benefit of the seller, even though implicitly it also denies the validity of the salam sale. And the seller claimed his right and benefit. And since the seller, i.e., the claimant, does not have proof, the statement of the denier, i.e., the buyer, is accepted. And its example is like in a mudarabah contract: the owner of capital said to the mudarib, 'I stipulated half the profit for you excluding ten dirhams,' i.e., specifically ten dirhams from the realized profit will be for me and the remaining profit will be divided equally between us; and the mudarib said, 'There was no condition of excluding ten dirhams; rather the condition was that the entire profit will be split equally between us.' In this case, the statement of the owner of capital is accepted because he denies the extra profit that the mudarib claims is his due. That is, the owner of capital specified ten dirhams for himself; the mudarib says he is an equal partner in them and his right is established in them, but the owner of capital denies this right of the mudarib, even though this denial and the exclusion of ten dirhams voids the mudarabah contract. Nevertheless, since he is denying an additional profit right of the mudarib upon himself, and the denier's statement is accepted when the claimant has no proof, in this case the statement of the denier, i.e., the owner of capital, is accepted. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that in the case of a salam contract, both the buyer and seller agree on one contract. Because salam is a binding contract. And denying any condition of salam renders the salam contract void; it does not become another contract. So in the mentioned issue, both agree on a single contract, but they differ on its validity and invalidity. In that the seller, by claiming a term, claims that the salam is valid, and the buyer, by denying the term, claims that it is invalid. And the apparent state of affairs witnesses that both agree on the validity of the salam, because both the buyer and seller are Muslims, and the apparent state of a Muslim requires that he avoid a void contract and engage in a valid contract. So from the buyer being Muslim, it is apparent that he also claims a valid contract, and he claims a valid contract when...

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the reverse scenario: the seller claims a term was agreed, but the buyer denies it. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the buyer's statement is accepted, drawing an analogy to a mudarabah case where the capital owner denies an additional profit condition. Their reasoning: the buyer is denying a right and benefit of the seller (the term), so he is the denier, and the denier's statement is accepted when the claimant has no proof. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the seller's statement is accepted. His proof: both parties agree on the same contract; the seller claims validity (by affirming the term), and the buyer claims invalidity. Since both are Muslims, their natural disposition is to enter valid contracts, so the apparent state supports the seller's claim of validity. Thus, the seller, as the one claiming validity, should have his statement accepted. The author notes that in the earlier scenario (seller denies term), the Sahibayn's position is consistent: there, the seller denies a right and benefit, so his statement is rejected. Here, the seller claims a right (the term), so according to Abu Hanifa, his claim of validity takes precedence.

Key Terms
صاحبین (ṣāḥibayn): Refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Imam Abu Hanifa.
مضاربت (muḍārabah): A profit-sharing partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, not a binding contract.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for future delivery of specified goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is the buyer's statement accepted in the reverse dispute where the seller claims a term?
A) Because the buyer is the one who paid the price.
B) Because the buyer is denying a right and benefit of the seller, making him the denier.
C) Because the buyer is a Muslim and thus presumed truthful.
D) Because the seller failed to deliver on time.

Key Takeaway
In the reverse salam term dispute, the Hanafi school has an internal ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa accepts the seller's statement (based on binding contract and apparent state), while the Sahibayn accept the buyer's statement (based on denier principle via qiyas to mudarabah).

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Dispute Over Stipulation of Term in Salam Contract
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whose statement is accepted when ajal is disputed in salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whose statement is accepted when a term is disputed in a salam contract, and explain why the qiyas to mudarabah is rejected.

Core Idea
When a term is disputed in salam, the buyer's denial is obstinate if beneficial to him; his statement is rejected.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on a procedural point: when the seller claims a delivery term was stipulated in a salam contract and the buyer denies it, whose word is taken? The Sahibayn analogize to mudarabah, where the denier's statement is accepted. Imam Abu Hanifa refutes the analogy by highlighting key differences between salam and mudarabah, leading to a general rule: one who denies something beneficial to himself is obstinate, and his statement is rejected.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the exact factual scenario: the seller (muslim ilayh) claims a term (ajal) was stipulated; the buyer (rabb al-salam) denies it. Both parties have no evidence. Whose statement is accepted?
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn.
2
Examine the Sahibayn's proof (qiyas to mudarabah)
The Sahibayn analogize to a mudarabah dispute: if the investor denies an exception clause, his statement is accepted as the denier. Similarly, in salam, the buyer denies the term, so his statement should be accepted.
The qiyas is based on a shared element: denial of a condition affecting profit.
3
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's critique (two differences)
Imam Abu Hanifa rejects the qiyas by pointing out two key differences: (a) In mudarabah, validity changes the contract type (valid=mudarabah, invalid=ijarah); in salam, the contract remains salam even if invalid. (b) Mudarabah is non-binding (ghayr lazim), so a dispute terminates it; salam is binding (lazim), so a dispute does not terminate it.
These differences make the analogy invalid (fasid al-i'tibar).
4
Apply the principle of 'denial after admission' and 'obstinacy'
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that by entering the contract, both parties implicitly admit the conditions of salam, including the term. The buyer's denial is a denial after admission, which is rejected. Moreover, the term benefits the buyer (keeps contract valid), so his denial is obstinate (ta'annot). The obstinate person's statement is rejected.
Thus the seller's statement is accepted, and the contract is valid with the term.
5
Extract the general maxim (qa'idah kulliyyah)
From this discussion, the text derives a general maxim: if a person's speech involves obstinacy by denying something beneficial to himself, then by consensus of the Hanafis his statement is rejected and his counterpart's is accepted.
This maxim has broader application beyond salam disputes.

باب السلم
اشرف البدایہ جلد نہم المال کا واپس کرنا لازم نہیں آیا تو اس انکار کی وجہ سے مسلم الیہ کے لئے پورے طور پر نفع بھی حاصل نہ ہوگا ہاں میعاد جو مسلم الیہ کا ظاہری حق اور نفع ہے اس کا انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے مسلم الیہ ضرور معنت اور سرکش ہوگا۔ اور معنت کا قول چونکہ مردود ہوتا ہے اس لیے مسلم الیہ کا قول مردود ہوگا۔ اور جب مسلم الیہ کا قول مردود ہوا تو رب السلام کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اس کے برخلاف اگر مسلم الیہ نے کہا کہ میں نے مسلم فیہ کے روی ہونے کی شرط لگائی تھی اور رب السلم نے کہا کہ مسلم فیہ کا کوئی وصف بیان نہیں کیا گیا تھا تو اس صورت میں مسلم الیہ کا قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ عدم بیان وصف کی وجہ سے بیع سلم کا فاسد ہونا یقینی اور غیر مختلف فیہ ہے اس لیے رب السلام بیان وصف کا انکار کر کے معنت اور متمرد شمار ہوگا۔ اور معنت ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا قول معتبر نہ ہوگا بلکہ مسلم الیہ کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر متن کا مسئلہ بر عکس ہو گیا یعنی مسلم الیہ نے میعاد کا دعوی کیا اور رب السلم نے انکار کیا تو صاحبین کے نزدیک رب السلم کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک مسلم الیہ کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ رب السلم ایک ایسے حق کا انکار کرتا ہے جو مسلم الیہ کا رب السلم پر ہے، یعنی مسلم فیہ ادا کرنے کے لیے میعاد کا ہونا مسلم الیہ کا حق ہے اور اس میں مسلم الیہ کا نفع بھی ہے بایں طور کہ مسلم الیہ اس مدت میں رأس المال سے خرید وفروخت کر کے اچھا خاصا نفع کما سکتا ہے پس رب السلام نے میعاد کا انکار کر کے مسلم الیہ کے مذکورہ حق اور نفع کا انکار کیا ہے اگر چہ اس کے ضمن میں بیع سلم صحیح ہونے کا انکار بھی ہو گیا اور مسلم الیہ نے اپنے اس حق اور نفع کا دعوی کیا ہے اور مسلم الیہ یعنی مدعی کے پاس چونکہ بینہ موجود نہیں ہے اس لیے منکر یعنی رب السلم کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اور اس کی مثال ایسی ہے جیسے عقد مضاربت میں رب المال نے مضارب سے کہا کہ میں نے تیرے لئے آدھے نفع کی شرط لگائی تھی علاوہ دس درہم کے یعنی حاصل شدہ نفع میں سے خاص طور پر دس درہم میرے لیے ہونگے اور باقی نفع دونوں کے درمیان آدھا آدھا ہوگا ۔ اور مضارب نے کہا کہ دس درہم کے استثناء کی کوئی شرط نہیں تھی بلکہ شرط یہ تھی کہ پورا نفع ہم دونوں کے درمیان نصف نصف ہوگا ، تو اس صورت میں رب المال کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے کیونکہ رب المال اس زیادتی نفع کا منکر ہے جس کا مضارب مستحق ہے یعنی رب المال نے جس دس دراہم کو اپنے لئے خاص کیا ہے مضارب کہتا ہے کہ میں ان میں بھی برابر کا شریک ہوں اور میرا حق ان میں بھی ثابت ہے مگر رب المال مضارب کے اس حق کا انکار کرتا ہے اگر چہ رب المال کے اس انکار اور دس درہم کے استثناء کی وجہ سے عقد مضاربت فاسد ہو جاتا ہے پس باوجود یکہ رب المال کے قول سے عقد مضاربت میں فساد لازم آتا ہے مگر چونکہ وہ اپنے اوپر مضارب کے لیے زیادتی نفع کا منکر ہے اور منکر کا قول مدعی کے پاس بینہ نہ ہونے کی صورت میں معتبر ہوتا ہے اس لیے اس صورت میں منکر یعنی رب المال کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عقد سلم کی صورت میں رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ دونوں ایک عقد پر متفق ہیں ۔ کیونکہ سلم ، عقد واحد ہے۔ اور شرائط سلم میں سے کسی شرط کا انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے عقد سلم فاسد ہو جاتا ہے دوسرا عقد نہیں ہو جاتا پس مسئلہ مذکورہ میں دونوں کا عقد واحد پر اتفاق ہوا لیکن صحت اور فساد میں اختلاف ہے اس طور پر کہ مسلم الیہ اجل اور میعاد کا دعوی کر کے سلم کے صحیح ہونے کا مدعی ہے اور رب السلم میعاد کا انکار کر کے اس کے فاسد ہونے کا مدعی ہے اور ظاہر حال اس پر شاہد ہے کہ دونوں سلم کے صحیح ہونے پر متفق ہوں کیونکہ رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ دونوں مسلمان ہیں اور مسلمان کا ظاہر حال اس کا متقاضی ہے کہ وہ عقد فاسد سے احتراز کرے۔ اور عقد صحیح کا ارتکاب کرے۔ پس رب السلم کے مسلمان ہونے سے بظاہر معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ وہ بھی عقد صحیح کا مدعی ہے اور وہ عقد صحیح کا مدعی اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جبکہ اس نے اجل اور میعاد کا اقرار کیا ہو تو گویا رب السلم نے صحت سلم کا اقرار کر کے اس کا انکار کیا ہے۔ اور کسی چیز کے اقرار کے بعد اس کا انکار مردود ہوتا ہے اس لیے رب السلم کی طرف سے میعاد کا اقرار کر کے سلم صحیح ہونے کا انکار کرنا مردود اور ناقابل قبول ہوگا۔ اور جب رب السلم کا میعاد کے انکار کا قول قبول نہ ہوا تو مسلم الیہ جو اجل اور میعاد کا مدعی ہے اس کا قول قبول ہوگا۔ اس کو آپ یوں بھی کہہ سکتے ہیں کہ رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ نے عقد سلم منعقد کرنے کا اقدام کیا ہے اور انسان جب کسی عقد پر اقدام کرتا ہے تو اس کے تمام شرائط کا التزام کرتا ہے اور میعاد بیع سلم کے شرائط میں سے ایک شرط ہے پس رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ کا عقد پر اقدام کرنا صحت سلم کا اقرار کرنا ہے مگر جب رب السلم نے میعاد کا انکار کیا تو گویا اقرار کے بعد انکار کیا اور اقرار کے بعد انکار چونکہ شرعاً مردود ہوتا ہے اس لیے رب السلم کا انکار اجل معتبر اور مقبول نہ ہوگا اور جب رب السلم کے انکار اجل کا قول معتبر نہ ہوا تو مسلم الیہ کے دعوی اجل کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ بخلاف المضاربة.. الخ سے صاحبین کے قیاس کا جواب ہے۔ جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ عقد سلم کو عقد مضاربت پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ ان دونوں میں بہت بڑا فرق ہے پہلا فرق تو یہ ہے کہ عقد مضاربت میں اختلاف کی صورت میں دونوں عقد کا واحد پر متفق نہ رہے۔ کیونکہ رب المال جس نے دس درہم کے استثناء کا دعویٰ کیا تھا یہ مضاربت کے فاسد ہونے کا مدعی ہے اور مضارب جس نے اس کا انکار کیا تھا وہ مضاربت کے صحیح ہونے کا مدعی ہے اور عقد مضاربت جب صحیح ہوتا ہے تو وہ عقد شرکت کہلاتا ہے اور جب فاسد ہوتا ہے تو اجارہ کہلاتا ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف عقد سلم کہ اس میں اختلاف کے باوجود رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ دونوں ایک عقد پر متفق رہتے ہیں صرف اتنی بات ہے کہ اجل اور میعاد کا منکر سلم فاسد کا مدعی ہوتا ہے اور اجل اور میعاد کا قائل سلم صحیح کا مدعی ہوتا ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ بیع سلم فاسد ہو کر بھی سلم ہی رہتی ہے دوسرا عقد نہیں بن جاتی اور مضاربت فاسد ہو کر مضاربت نہیں رہتی بلکہ اجارہ ہو جاتی ہے۔ دوسرا فرق یہ ہے کہ عقد مضاربت عقد لازم نہیں ہے کیونکہ عقد مضاربت کے بعد، مضارب اور رب المال دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کو فسخ کر دینے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے اور عقد سلم لازم ہوتا ہے، چنانچہ رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ دونوں میں سے ایک کے فسخ کرنے سے عقد سلم فسخ نہیں ہوتا ہے بلکہ دونوں کی رضامندی ضروری ہے۔ پس جب عقد سلم اور عقد مضاربت کے درمیان اتنا فرق ہے تو عقد سلم کو عقد مضاربت پر قیاس کرنا کس طرح درست ہوگا۔ رہی یہ بات کہ عقد مضاربت کی صورت میں رب المال کا قول کیوں قبول ہوتا ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ عقد مضاربت جب غیر لازم ہے تو رب المال اور مضارب کے اختلاف سے عقد مرتفع ہو جائے گا اور جب عقد مرتفع ہو گیا تو ان کا اختلاف بھی معتبر نہ ہوگا بلکہ فقط رب المال کے مال میں مضارب کا نفع کے حقدار ہونے کا دعوی باقی رہا یعنی مضارب اس بات کا مدعی ہے کہ رب المال کے مال میں میرا بھی نفع ہے اور رب المال اس کا منکر ہے اور مدعی یعنی مضارب کے پاس چونکہ بینہ موجود نہیں اس لیے منکر یعنی رب المال کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ اور عقد سلم چونکہ لازم ہے اس لیے رب السلم اور مسلم الیہ کے اختلاف سے عقد سلم مرتفع نہ ہو گا اور جب عقد سلم مرتفع نہیں ہوا تو رب السلم جس نے میعاد کا انکار کیا ہے گویا وہ فساد سلم کا مدعی ہے۔ اور فساد سلم کا مدعی چونکہ متعنت اور سرکش ہوتا ہے کیونکہ میعاد کا انکار کرنے میں اس کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے اس لئے رب السلام معنت ہوگا اور متعنت کا قول چونکہ مردود ہوتا ہے اور اس کے ساتھی کا قول مقبول ہوتا ہے اس لیے رب السلم کا قول مردود اور مسلم الیہ کا قول مقبول اور معتبر ہو گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس پوری تقریر سے ایک قاعدہ کلیہ مستنبط ہوا وہ یہ کہ اگر کسی کا کلام تعنت اور سرکشی پر مشتمل ہو بایں طور کہ اس چیز کا انکار کرتا ہو جو اس کے لیے نافع اور سودمند ہے تو باتفاق احناف اس کا قول مردود اور اس کے ساتھی کا کلام معتبر ہوگا ۔ اور اگر کسی کا
Chapter of Salam. Ashraf al-Hidayah Volume 9: If returning the capital is not obligatory, then due to that denial, the Muslim ilayh will not gain profit entirely; yes, the term which is the apparent right and profit of the Muslim ilayh, by denying it the Muslim ilayh becomes definitely obstinate and rebellious. And since the statement of the obstinate is rejected, the statement of the Muslim ilayh is rejected. And when the statement of the Muslim ilayh is rejected, the statement of Rabb al-Salam is accepted. Conversely, if the Muslim ilayh said: 'I stipulated the existence of the muslam fihi' and Rabb al-Salam said: 'No description of the muslam fihi was mentioned', then in this case the statement of the Muslim ilayh is accepted because due to lack of description, the salam sale becoming invalid is certain and undisputed; therefore Rabb al-Salam, by denying the description, is considered obstinate and defiant. And due to being obstinate, his statement is not accepted, rather the statement of the Muslim ilayh is accepted. The author of al-Hidayah says: If the issue of the text becomes opposite, i.e., the Muslim ilayh claims the term and Rabb al-Salam denies, then according to the Sahibayn the statement of Rabb al-Salam is accepted, and according to Imam Abu Hanifa the statement of the Muslim ilayh is accepted. The proof of the Sahibayn is that Rabb al-Salam denies a right that the Muslim ilayh has upon Rabb al-Salam, i.e., the existence of a term for delivering the muslam fihi is the right of the Muslim ilayh, and therein is profit for the Muslim ilayh in that the Muslim ilayh can trade with the capital during that period and earn considerable profit. So Rabb al-Salam, by denying the term, has denied the aforementioned right and profit of the Muslim ilayh, even though by implication he also denies the validity of the salam sale; and the Muslim ilayh has claimed this right and profit. And since the Muslim ilayh, the claimant, has no evidence, therefore the statement of the denier, i.e., Rabb al-Salam, is accepted. An example of this is in a mudarabah contract: Rabb al-Mal said to the mudarib: 'I stipulated for you half the profit except ten dirhams', i.e., from the profit earned, specifically ten dirhams are for me, and the remaining profit is half-half between us. And the mudarib said: 'There was no condition of exception of ten dirhams; rather the condition was that the entire profit is half-half between us.' In this case, the statement of Rabb al-Mal is accepted because Rabb al-Mal is the denier of the extra profit that the mudarib claims; i.e., Rabb al-Mal has specifically allocated those ten dirhams to himself, the mudarib says he is an equal partner in them and his right is established in them, but Rabb al-Mal denies this right of the mudarib. Even though due to this denial and exception of ten dirhams the mudarabah contract becomes invalid (fasid), yet because he is the denier of the extra profit owed to the mudarib, and the statement of the denier when the claimant has no evidence is accepted, therefore in this case the statement of the denier, i.e., Rabb al-Mal, is accepted. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that in a salam contract, both Rabb al-Salam and the Muslim ilayh agree on one contract because salam is a single contract. And by denying any condition of the conditions of salam, the salam contract becomes invalid (fasid) but does not become another contract. So in the mentioned issue, both agree on a single contract but differ regarding validity and invalidity. The Muslim ilayh, by claiming the term, claims the validity of salam, and Rabb al-Salam, by denying the term, claims its invalidity. And the apparent situation witnesses that both agree on the validity of salam because both are Muslims, and the apparent state of a Muslim requires that he avoid an invalid contract and undertake a valid one. So from the fact that Rabb al-Salam is a Muslim, it appears that he also claims a valid contract, and he can only claim a valid contract if he has admitted the term. So it is as if Rabb al-Salam has admitted the validity of salam and then denied it. And denial after admission is rejected; therefore Rabb al-Salam's denial of the term and thus the validity of salam is rejected and unacceptable. And when Rabb al-Salam's statement of denial of the term is not accepted, then the statement of the Muslim ilayh, who claims the term, is accepted. You can also say: Rabb al-Salam and the Muslim ilayh have undertaken to conclude a salam contract, and when a person undertakes a contract, he commits to all its conditions; and the term is one of the conditions of salam sale. So their undertaking the contract is an admission of the validity of salam. But when Rabb al-Salam denies the term, it is as if he denies after admission, and denial after admission is legally rejected, so Rabb al-Salam's denial of the term is not accepted and not admissible. And when Rabb al-Salam's denial of the term is not accepted, then the claim of the term by the Muslim ilayh is accepted. The phrase 'contrary to mudarabah etc.' is a response to the analogy of the Sahibayn. The gist of the response is that it is not correct to analogize a salam contract to a mudarabah contract because there is a great difference between the two. The first difference is that in a mudarabah contract, when there is a dispute, the two parties do not agree on a single contract. Because Rabb al-Mal who claimed the exception of ten dirhams is claiming the invalidity of mudarabah, and the mudarib who denied it is claiming the validity of mudarabah. And when mudarabah is valid, it is called a contract of partnership, and when invalid, it is called a contract of hire (ijarah). In contrast, in a salam contract, despite the dispute, both Rabb al-Salam and the Muslim ilayh remain agreed on a single contract; the only thing is that the denier of the term is claiming invalid salam, and the proponent of the term is claiming valid salam. The conclusion is that a salam sale, even if invalid, remains a salam sale and does not become another contract, while mudarabah, when invalid, does not remain mudarabah but becomes ijarah. The second difference is that a mudarabah contract is not a binding contract (lazim) because after the mudarabah contract, each of the mudarib and Rabb al-Mal has the option to rescind, whereas a salam contract is binding; thus, if one of them rescinds, the salam contract is not rescinded without the consent of both. So when there is such a difference between salam and mudarabah, how can it be valid to analogize salam to mudarabah? As for why in the mudarabah case the statement of Rabb al-Mal is accepted, the answer is that since mudarabah is non-binding, the contract is terminated by their dispute. And when the contract is terminated, their dispute about the contract is not considered; rather only the claim of the mudarib regarding his right to profit in the property of Rabb al-Mal remains. The mudarib claims that he has a share in Rabb al-Mal's property, and Rabb al-Mal denies it, and since the mudarib has no evidence, the statement of the denier, i.e., Rabb al-Mal, is accepted. And because the salam contract is binding, it is not terminated by their dispute. And when the salam contract is not terminated, then Rabb al-Salam, who denied the term, is effectively claiming the invalidity of salam. And the one who claims the invalidity of salam is obstinate and rebellious because there is no benefit to him in denying the term. Therefore Rabb al-Salam is obstinate, and the statement of the obstinate is rejected, and the statement of his counterpart is accepted. So Rabb al-Salam's statement is rejected and the Muslim ilayh's statement is accepted and valid. The author of al-Hidayah says: From this entire discussion a general maxim is derived: If a person's speech involves obstinacy and rebellion, in that he denies something that is beneficial and advantageous to him, then by consensus of the Hanafis his statement is rejected and his companion's statement is accepted. And if someone's...

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a salam (forward sale) contract when the two parties disagree on whether a term (ajal/miqad) was stipulated. The specific scenario: the seller (Muslim ilayh) claims the term was stipulated, and the buyer (Rabb al-Salam) denies it. The text first presents the initial ruling from the matn (likely from Al-Hidayah): if the seller (Muslim ilayh) is the one denying the term (i.e., he claims no term was stipulated while the buyer says it was), the seller's statement is rejected because he is being obstinate (mu'annit) by denying something beneficial to him. Then the opposite scenario is considered: if the seller claims the term was stipulated and the buyer denies, then the two disciples (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) rule in favor of the buyer's statement being accepted, while Imam Abu Hanifa rules in favor of the seller's statement. The commentary explains each side's reasoning. The Sahibayn analogize salam to mudarabah: in mudarabah, if the investor (Rabb al-Mal) denies an exception clause, his statement is accepted because he is the denier of a right claimed by the mudarib. Similarly here, Rabb al-Salam denies the term, which is a right of the Muslim ilayh, so his statement is accepted. Imam Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that both parties agreed to a single contract (salam), and the validity of salam requires the term. The buyer, by denying the term, is effectively denying the validity of the contract after having admitted it by entering into it. Denial after admission is invalid. Moreover, the buyer's denial is obstinate because the term benefits both parties—the seller gets profit from trading, and the contract remains valid. The text then refutes the analogy to mudarabah by highlighting two key differences: (1) In mudarabah, validity or invalidity changes the contract type (valid = partnership; invalid = hire), whereas salam remains salam even if invalid. (2) Mudarabah is non-binding (ghayr lazim), so a dispute terminates the contract; salam is binding (lazim), so a dispute does not terminate it. Thus in salam, the buyer's denial is considered obstinacy because he denies something beneficial to himself (the validity of the contract). The passage concludes with a general rule: anyone who denies something beneficial to themselves is obstinate, and their statement is rejected by consensus of the Hanafis.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A forward sale where payment is made in advance (cash) for goods to be delivered later, with stipulation of exact description, quantity, and delivery time.
اجل (ajal): The specified term/time for delivery of the muslam fihi (subject matter) in a salam contract.
مضاربت (mudarabah): A partnership contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profits shared according to agreement.
تعنت (ta'annot): Obstinacy or rebelliousness; a person who denies something that is beneficial to him without reason.
قاعدة كلية (qa'idah kulliyyah): A general maxim derived by the author: if a person's speech involves obstinacy (denying what benefits him), his statement is rejected and his counterpart's is accepted by consensus of the Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the buyer's denial of the term in a salam contract considered obstinate?
A) Because the term benefits the seller only.
B) Because the term is a condition that benefits the buyer by keeping the contract valid.
C) Because the buyer is the denier.
D) Because the seller has evidence.

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi dispute on term in salam: Imam Abu Hanifa accepts seller's statement, Sahibayn accept buyer's; the mufta bihi is Imam Abu Hanifa's view based on the principle that denying what is beneficial is obstinacy.
